It is a data-driven approach that aims to uncover the underlying dynamics and relationships within a system.When comparing EDM to other methods for matching experimental data, it's important to note that EDM takes a different approach. Traditional methods, such as fitting mathematical models to data or using statistical techniques, often require a priori knowledge of the system dynamics and assume specific functional forms. In contrast, EDM is a non-parametric method that does not rely on preconceived assumptions about the system.EDM handles cases where experimental data is limited or incomplete by utilizing the concept of time-delay embedding. Time-delay embedding reconstructs the system's phase space using time-delayed copies of the available data. By creating a higher-dimensional representation of the system, EDM can extract meaningful information even from limited or incomplete data. This approach allows EDM to capture the system's dynamics and predict future behavior, even in cases where only a few variables or measurements are available.An example of how EDM has been used in a real-world application is in the field of ecology. Scientists have used EDM to study and model the dynamics of ecological systems, such as predator-prey interactions or population dynamics. By analyzing time series data of species abundances, researchers have applied EDM to uncover the underlying ecological mechanisms, identify critical transitions, and predict future population dynamics. This data-driven approach has provided insights into the complex dynamics of ecosystems and has the potential to inform conservation and management strategies.1 / 1EDM (Empirical Dynamic Modeling) is a data-driven modeling approach that aims to capture the underlying dynamics of a system directly from its observed behavior. Compared to other methods for matching experimental data, such as mechanistic modeling or statistical modeling, EDM has several advantages. 

Firstly, EDM does not require explicit knowledge of the underlying mechanisms governing the system being studied. This means that EDM can be applied to systems for which detailed mechanistic understanding is lacking or incomplete. 

Secondly, EDM does not make assumptions about the statistical distribution of the data, which can be a limitation of traditional statistical models. Instead, EDM is based on the assumption that the observed data is generated by a low-dimensional dynamical system, and seeks to identify the parameters of this system from the data.

Thirdly, EDM is a flexible approach that can handle a wide variety of data types, including time series data, spatial data, and high-dimensional data. This makes it a powerful tool for modeling complex systems with diverse data sources.

In cases where experimental data is limited or incomplete, EDM can still be used to infer the underlying dynamics of the system. In particular, EDM can be applied to partially observed systems, where only a subset of the system variables is measured, or to noisy data, where the measurements are subject to random fluctuations or measurement errors. In these cases, the inferred models may be less accurate or have higher uncertainty, but can still provide valuable insights into the system's behavior.

An example of how EDM has been used in a real-world application is in the study of ecological systems. For instance, researchers have used EDM to model the dynamics of marine ecosystems and to predict the effects of climate change on these systems. In one study, researchers used EDM to model the dynamics of phytoplankton populations in the North Atlantic, based on satellite data on ocean color and temperature. The resulting model was able to capture the seasonal and inter-annual variability of the phytoplankton populations, and to predict changes in these populations under different climate scenarios. This demonstrates the potential of EDM to provide insights into complex ecological systems and to inform management and conservation efforts.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTApologies for the confusion in my previous response. **Authors:** Andrew D. White, James F. Dama, and Gregory A. Voth

**Publication Date:** April 30, 2015

**DOI:** [10.1021/acs.jctc.5b00178](https://doi.org/10.1021/acs.jctc.5b00178)

**Summary:**

In molecular modeling, creating models that align with experimental data is crucial. This is often achieved by iteratively tuning the molecular force field in simulations. An alternative approach involves biasing simulations, resulting in hybrid models composed of the original force field and biasing terms. The authors previously introduced an experiment directed simulation (EDS) method that minimally biases simulations to match average values. 

In this paper, a new method called experiment directed metadynamics (EDM) is introduced, which creates minimal biases for matching entire free energy surfaces, such as radial distribution functions and phi/psi angle free energies. EDM allows for a tunable mixture of experimental data and the free energy of the unbiased ensemble with explicit ratios. The method is proven to be convergent, and a maximum entropy argument proves that the final bias is minimal and unique.

Examples of its use include constructing ensembles that follow a desired free energy. The example systems studied are a Lennard-Jones fluid to match a radial distribution function, an atomistic model augmented with bioinformatics data, and a three-component electrolyte solution where ab initio simulation data is used to improve a classical empirical model.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThank you for providing additional information about another meaning of EDM in the context of molecular modeling. Compared to other methods for matching simulations to experimental data, EDM has several advantages and limitations.

One traditional approach for matching simulations to experimental data is to use empirical force fields, which are mathematical functions that describe the interactions between atoms in a molecule. Empirical force fields are often fitted to experimental data, such as molecular geometries, vibrational frequencies, or thermodynamic properties, through a process of parameterization. 

However, empirical force fields can suffer from limitations, such as overfitting to the training data, transferability issues, and the inability to accurately represent complex physical phenomena. Additionally, empirical force fields may not be able to capture the full complexity of the underlying system, especially for systems that have limited or incomplete experimental data.

Another approach for matching simulations to experimental data is to use quantum mechanical (QM) calculations, which provide a more accurate description of the system's electronic structure than empirical force fields. However, QM calculations can be computationally expensive, especially for large systems, and may not be feasible for many practical applications.

EDM, on the other hand, provides a flexible and efficient approach for matching simulations to experimental data. EDM combines the accuracy of experimental data with the flexibility of molecular simulations, allowing researchers to create hybrid models that accurately represent the underlying system. EDM can be applied to a wide range of systems and data types, including radial distribution functions, phi/psi angle free energies, and even ab initio simulation data. 

However, EDM also has some limitations. For example, as with any simulation-based approach, the accuracy of the results depends on the quality of the initial simulation model and the quality of the experimental data. Additionally, EDM requires a significant amount of computational resources, especially for large systems or complex data types.

Overall, EDM represents a powerful and promising approach for matching simulations to experimental data, with the potential to improve our understanding of complex biological and chemical systems.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTApologies for the confusion caused by the multiple meanings of "EDM." Experiment directed metadynamics plugin for lammps or someday other simulation engines. Experiment directed metadynamics is a method to morph a molecular dynamics simulation to follow a desired free energy surface (probability distribution). See this paper This plugin allows morphing pairwise interactions and coordinate dimensions. These two collective variables are CPU intensive and difficult on large systems, so electronic-dance-music uses a scalable MPI-based approach to this.

What is unique to this implementation of metadynamics is that it allows bias limiting. Generally in metadynamics, the bias added to a system is a small amount. However, when biasing pairwise interactions or coordinates of atoms there are many hills that can be added per bias update step. Even if you choose to only add some, a large amount of bias may be added. Electronic dance music solves this problem by limiting the amount of energy added to the system per bias update. You can't just stop adding hills after a certain amount of bias has been added in a step, since that creates sample bias. So instead the hills that should be added, but would add too much energy, are buffered and added later.

You can choose how many hills to add per bias update, a desired amount of energy to add to the bias per update step, and an upper limit to the amount of bias that can be added.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe electronic dance music (EDM) implementation of experiment directed metadynamics (EDM) in the context of molecular simulations introduces unique features and optimizations. Here are the key aspects of this implementation:Morphing Interactions and Coordinates: The EDM plugin for molecular dynamics simulations, such as LAMMPS, allows for the morphing or biasing of pairwise interactions and coordinate dimensions. These collective variables can significantly impact the system's behavior and are often computationally intensive to simulate, especially for large systems.Scalable MPI-Based Approach: Electronic dance music utilizes a scalable Message Passing Interface (MPI)-based approach to handle the computationally demanding task of morphing interactions and coordinates. MPI enables efficient parallelization and distribution of calculations across multiple computational resources, allowing for improved performance and scalability.Bias Limiting: A unique aspect of this implementation is the incorporation of bias limiting. In traditional metadynamics, the bias added to the system is typically small. However, when biasing pairwise interactions or atom coordinates, there can be numerous hills added per bias update step. This can potentially introduce a significant amount of bias, leading to unwanted artifacts in the sampling.To address this issue, EDM introduces a mechanism for limiting the amount of energy added to the system per bias update. It avoids simply stopping the addition of hills after a certain amount of bias has been added to prevent sample bias. Instead, EDM buffers the hills that would contribute excessive energy and adds them later in a controlled manner.Customizable Parameters: The EDM implementation provides flexibility through customizable parameters. Users can define the number of hills to add per bias update, the desired amount of energy to add to the bias per update step, and an upper limit on the amount of bias that can be added. These parameters allow users to fine-tune the simulation to match their specific requirements and balance the biasing effects.In summary, the EDM implementation of experiment directed metadynamics offers optimizations and unique features for morphing interactions and coordinates in molecular simulations. It employs an MPI-based approach for scalability and introduces bias limiting to control the amount of energy added to the system per bias update. Customizable parameters further enhance the flexibility of the implementation.1 / 1Thank you for the additional information. It seems that in this context, "EDM" refers to "Electronic Dance Music" rather than "Experiment Directed Metadynamics." 

The plugin you describe is an implementation of metadynamics that allows for the morphing of a molecular dynamics simulation to follow a desired free energy surface. This is achieved by adding a bias to the simulation that is updated over time based on the difference between the current simulation and the desired free energy surface. 

One unique feature of this implementation is the ability to limit the amount of bias added to the system per update step, which can prevent the introduction of sample bias. This is achieved by buffering hills that would add too much energy and adding them later, ensuring that the bias is added gradually and without introducing artifacts. 

The plugin also allows for the customization of several parameters, such as the number of hills added per update, the desired amount of energy added per update, and an upper limit to the amount of bias that can be added. This makes the implementation flexible and adaptable to a wide range of simulation scenarios.

Thank you for clarifying the intended meaning and providing the accurate description of the plugin's functionality and features in the context of metadynamics.1 / 1Install
It only works with lammps right now. To install:

Create a build directory from the EDM source dir
cd into build and run cmake ..
sudo make install to install the EDM library locally
(Optional) manuall edit the CMakeLists.txt and set the LAMMPS_SRC variable to the LAMMPS src dir on your machine
make patch to move the EDM LAMMPS files. If you did not perform step 4, you must instead use make patch -DLAMMPS_SRC="path/to/your/LAMMPS/src"
Now go to your LAMMPS src dir and run make yes-user-edm and make-<machine> as normal.
Ignore all the CMake files floating aruond in the source, they're just for unit tests.

Lammps Usage
Pairwise EDM
This is for running experiment directed metadynamics on pairs, for example to match a radial distribution function. Please see the plumed_grids for converting a radial distribution function into a probability distribution function.

Pairwise EDM Fix
Your command should be:

fix [fix-ID] [group-ID] edm_pair [temperature] [edm-input-file] [hill addition stride (integer)] [write bias stride (integer)] [edm input bias/target file] [random number seed] [rdf pairs]
With:

fix-ID: This is the ID for the fix that will be created
group-ID: The group of atoms on which the fix will be done. Should contain the types that are referred to in the rdf pairs argument
temperature: The temperature used for the bias. Almost always same as simulation temperature.
hill addition stride: How many timesteps between hills adds
write bias stride: How often to write the bias. Writing the bias takes a long long time, so make this large. Each time this stride is reached, a lammps tabular potential is written out containing the current bias (edm_pair only) and a histogram of the collective variable is written.
edm output bias file: This is where the bias being applied will be written
random number seed: Some digits to seed the random number generator
rdf pairs: Just like the lammps rdf command, this should be either asterisks (match any type) or integer types. Like 2 * means match type 2 atoms in the given group-ID with anything.
The decision on what goes into the edm-input-file and what goes into the lammps fix were arbitrary and follows no logical reasons.

Coordinate EDM Fix
Everything is the same as above, except no rdf pair keyword needs to be added.

EDM Input File
Here's an example EDM input file demonstrating all options:

//logical - indicates tempering or not
tempering		1

//If this is set to 0, then global tempering will be used. If this is
//non-zero, then thresholding will be used. If this is not set, local
//tempering is used
global_tempering	2.0

//hill height/prefactor
hill_prefactor		0.02 

//can control how much bias is added to the system in a given
//step. This is an upper limit which will not be exceeded. If this is
//not set, it's assumed to be the same as the hill prefactor.
bias_per_step 0.1

//This is not a bias restart, but the target that EDM will convert the
//PMF into. The file should contain your target as -ln(P(s)), where P(s)
//is the probability of the collective variable s
target_filename	       li_oc_target.dat

//The bias factor for well-tempering. Required if tempering is 1
bias_factor 		5.0

//The dimension of the bias. 
dimension 		1

//Hills added per step. 
hill_density		250

//In case you are targeting only a part of the PMF. For multiple
//dimensions use spaces between the items
box_low			1.68
box_high		5.0

//The grid spacing for bias calculations
bias_spacing		0.00025

//The gaussian standard deviation for the basis gaussians of the
//system
bias_sigma		0.025

//where to write hills
hills_filename		lioc_hills

//an initial bias to begin with 
initial_bias_filename   restart_bias.dat

//where to write a histogram of the CV.
//This is useful ensuring the target is reached and analyzing the PMF
//NOTE: The histogram is reset every time the bias file is rewritten.
//It is not a running histogram.

histogram_filename	cv_hist.dat
Boundaries
Electronic-dance-music uses the McGovern-De Pablo-boundary-corrected and zero-force hills described in the original EDM paper in 1 dimension. In more than 1, YOUR TARGET SHOULD ENCOMPASS THE WHOLE REGION. Electronic-dance-music doesn't check for this, but you will get CRAZY boundary behavior if you have a partial bias.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThank you for providing the instructions for installing and using the EDM plugin for LAMMPS. If you skipped step 4, use make patch -DLAMMPS_SRC="path/to/your/LAMMPS/src" instead.LAMMPS Usage for Pairwise EDM Fix:
To use the pairwise EDM fix in LAMMPS, use the following command:cssCopy codefix [fix-ID] [group-ID] edm_pair [temperature] [edm-input-file] [hill addition stride] [write bias stride] [edm input bias/target file] [random number seed] [rdf pairs]
Here's an explanation of the parameters:fix-ID: The ID for the fix that will be created.group-ID: The group of atoms on which the fix will be applied. Boundaries:
The implementation handles boundaries using the McGovern-De Pablo boundary correction and zero-force hills in 1 dimension. For more than 1 dimension, it's important to ensure that the target encompasses the entire region. Failure to do so may result in boundary artifacts.These instructions and usage details should help you install and use the EDM plugin for LAMMPS effectively.1 / 1Thank you for the detailed instructions on how to install and use the Electronic Dance Music (EDM) plugin for LAMMPS. By leveraging this capability, researchers can gain valuable insights into complex molecular systems and enhance our understanding of their behavior.If you have any further questions or need additional assistance, please feel free to ask.1 / 1Can you explain how the McGovern-De Pablo method works?1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe McGovern-De Pablo method is a technique used to correct for boundary effects in metadynamics simulations, particularly in systems with finite simulation boxes. In metadynamics, bias is added to the system to explore the free energy landscape. However, when working with finite simulation boxes, the system can experience boundary artifacts that distort the calculated free energy surface.The McGovern-De Pablo method aims to mitigate these artifacts by introducing zero-force hills near the system boundaries. These zero-force hills effectively act as "walls" that prevent biased exploration beyond the boundaries of the simulation box.Here's a simplified explanation of how the McGovern-De Pablo method works:Determining System Boundaries: The first step is to identify the boundaries of the simulation box in the relevant collective variables or coordinates. These boundaries can be determined based on the specific system being studied.Defining the Boundary Regions: Once the system boundaries are determined, boundary regions are defined on each side of the box. These regions typically have a width of a few times the characteristic length scale of the system.Introducing Zero-Force Hills: Zero-force hills are added within the boundary regions. These hills have a flat top and decay to zero force away from the boundary. The shape and width of the zero-force hills are typically determined based on the characteristics of the system.Biasing the System: The metadynamics bias is applied to the system as usual, with Gaussian hills added in the collective variables of interest. However, when a biased simulation approaches the boundaries, the zero-force hills effectively limit the biased exploration in those regions.By introducing the zero-force hills, the McGovern-De Pablo method prevents excessive biasing and exploration beyond the simulation box boundaries. This helps to reduce boundary artifacts and ensures a more accurate representation of the system's free energy landscape.It's important to note that the specific implementation and details of the McGovern-De Pablo method may vary depending on the system and the collective variables used. However, the underlying idea remains consistent: adding zero-force hills near the boundaries to mitigate boundary effects in metadynamics simulations.1 / 1McGovern-de Pablo Boundary Corrected Hills
Metadynamics is a way of overcoming energetic barriers in molecular simulation (Laio & Parrinello, 2002). Itâs part of a class of algorithms that focus on making rare-events more frequent in simulations. Itâs been getting quite popular: it even has itâs own wikipedia page. When performing metadynamics, a bias potential is added to the simulation to enhance the sampling of rare events. The bias potential is typically constructed by adding Gaussian-shaped hills to the free energy surface as a function of one or more collective variables. However, when the collective variable(s) being biased approach the boundary of the simulation box, the hills can become distorted and introduce artifacts into the simulation.

The McGovern-De Pablo method corrects for these boundary effects by modifying the shape of the hills near the edges of the simulation box. Specifically, the method adds an additional term to the bias potential that depends on the distance of each atom from the simulation box edge. The additional term has the effect of reducing the height of the hills near the boundary, which helps to prevent the distortion of the hills and improve the accuracy of the simulation.

The McGovern-De Pablo method is based on the assumption that the hills added to the bias potential are zero-force, meaning that the force on the atoms due to the bias potential is zero at the top of the hill. This assumption allows for a simple correction that can be applied to the hills near the boundary, without requiring any additional information about the system or the free energy surface.

Overall, the McGovern-De Pablo method provides a simple and effective technique for correcting the boundary effects that can arise when using metadynamics on a finite simulation box. The method is widely used in molecular simulations and has been shown to improve the accuracy of free energy calculations, especially for systems with complex or irregular boundaries.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThank you for providing a detailed explanation of the McGovern-De Pablo method for correcting boundary effects in metadynamics simulations. This adjustment helps to prevent distortion and artifacts, improving the accuracy of the simulation and the reliability of free energy calculations.The simplicity of the correction, based on the assumption of zero-force hills, allows for straightforward implementation without requiring extensive knowledge of the system or the underlying free energy surface.Overall, the McGovern-De Pablo method is a valuable technique in mitigating boundary effects and enhancing the accuracy of metadynamics simulations, particularly in systems with finite simulation boxes and complex boundaries.Thank you for clarifying and providing an accurate description of the McGovern-De Pablo method. Metadynamics (MTD; also abbreviated as METAD or MetaD) is a computer simulation method in computational physics, chemistry and biology. It is used to estimate the free energy and other state functions of a system, where ergodicity is hindered by the form of the system's energy landscape. It was first suggested by Alessandro Laio and Michele Parrinello in 2002[1] and is usually applied within molecular dynamics simulations. MTD closely resembles a number of recent methods such as adaptively biased molecular dynamics,[2] adaptive reaction coordinate forces[3] and local elevation umbrella sampling.[4] More recently, both the original and well-tempered metadynamics[5] were derived in the context of importance sampling and shown to be a special case of the adaptive biasing potential setting.[6] MTD is related to the WangâLandau sampling.[7]1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTMetadynamics (MTD), also known as METAD or MetaD, is a computer simulation method used in computational physics, chemistry, and biology to estimate the free energy and other state functions of a system. It is particularly useful when the system's energy landscape hinders ergodicity, which refers to the ability of a system to explore all possible states.MTD was first proposed by Alessandro Laio and Michele Parrinello in 2002. It is commonly applied within molecular dynamics simulations. The method shares similarities with other recent techniques such as adaptively biased molecular dynamics, adaptive reaction coordinate forces, and local elevation umbrella sampling. Both the original metadynamics and well-tempered metadynamics have been derived in the context of importance sampling and have been shown to be a special case of the adaptive biasing potential setting. MTD is also related to the Wang-Landau sampling method.The main goal of metadynamics is to enhance the sampling of rare events in the system by introducing a bias potential that evolves during the simulation. This bias potential helps to overcome energy barriers and promotes the exploration of different regions of the system's energy landscape. By continuously adding Gaussian-shaped hills to the bias potential as a function of one or more collective variables, metadynamics encourages the system to explore a wide range of configurations.Metadynamics has proven to be a valuable tool in studying complex systems and has applications in various fields, including materials science, drug discovery, and protein folding. It allows researchers to estimate free energy differences between different states of a system, providing insights into thermodynamic properties and reaction mechanisms.Please note that the information provided here is a summary from Wikipedia and may not encompass the entirety of the topic. For more detailed information, I recommend referring to the original research papers or specialized literature on metadynamics.1 / 1Introduction
The technique builds on a large number of related methods including (in a chronological order) the deflation,[8] tunneling,[9] tabu search,[10] local elevation,[11] conformational flooding,[12] Engkvist-KarlstrÃ¶m[13] and Adaptive Biasing Force methods.[14]

Metadynamics has been informally described as "filling the free energy wells with computational sand".[15] The algorithm assumes that the system can be described by a few collective variables (CV). During the simulation, the location of the system in the space determined by the collective variables is calculated and a positive Gaussian potential is added to the real energy landscape of the system. In this way the system is discouraged to come back to the previous point. During the evolution of the simulation, more and more Gaussians sum up, thus discouraging more and more the system to go back to its previous steps, until the system explores the full energy landscapeâat this point the modified free energy becomes a constant as a function of the collective variables which is the reason for the collective variables to start fluctuating heavily. At this point the energy landscape can be recovered as the opposite of the sum of all Gaussians.

The time interval between the addition of two Gaussian functions, as well as the Gaussian height and Gaussian width, are tuned to optimize the ratio between accuracy and computational cost. By simply changing the size of the Gaussian, metadynamics can be fitted to yield very quickly a rough map of the energy landscape by using large Gaussians, or can be used for a finer grained description by using smaller Gaussians.[1] Usually, the well-tempered metadynamics[5] is used to change the Gaussian size adaptively.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTMetadynamics is a simulation technique that builds upon various related methods, such as deflation, tunneling, tabu search, local elevation, conformational flooding, Engkvist-KarlstrÃ¶m, and Adaptive Biasing Force methods. These methods have contributed to the development of metadynamics and have influenced its evolution over time.The concept of metadynamics is often informally described as "filling the free energy wells with computational sand." The technique assumes that the system under study can be described using a small set of collective variables (CV). During the simulation, the system's position in the space defined by these collective variables is calculated, and a positive Gaussian potential is added to the actual energy landscape of the system. This addition discourages the system from returning to its previous state. As the simulation progresses, more Gaussians are added, further discouraging the system from retracing its steps. This process continues until the system explores the entire energy landscape, at which point the modified free energy becomes constant with respect to the collective variables, causing them to exhibit significant fluctuations. At this stage, the original energy landscape can be recovered by taking the opposite of the sum of all the added Gaussians.The time interval between the addition of two Gaussian functions, as well as the height and width of the Gaussians, are carefully adjusted to optimize the trade-off between accuracy and computational cost. By modifying the size of the Gaussian functions, metadynamics can provide a quick, coarse-grained map of the energy landscape using large Gaussians, or a more detailed, fine-grained description using smaller Gaussians. The well-tempered metadynamics variant is commonly employed to adaptively adjust the size of the Gaussians.Metadynamics is a powerful method for exploring complex energy landscapes, estimating free energy differences, and studying rare events in molecular systems. Its versatility and effectiveness have made it widely used in various fields, including computational physics, chemistry, and biology.Please note that the information provided here is a summary and should not be considered exhaustive. For a more comprehensive understanding, I recommend referring to the original research papers and specialized literature on metadynamics.1 / 1Metadynamics has the advantage, upon methods like adaptive umbrella sampling, of not requiring an initial estimate of the energy landscape to explore.[1] However, it is not trivial to choose proper collective variables for a complex simulation. Typically, it requires several trials to find a good set of collective variables, but there are several automatic procedure proposed: essential coordinates,[17] Sketch-Map,[18] and non-linear data-driven collective variables.[19]1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIndeed, one of the advantages of metadynamics is that it does not require an initial estimate of the energy landscape to explore, distinguishing it from methods like adaptive umbrella sampling. This characteristic makes metadynamics particularly useful when dealing with complex systems.However, selecting appropriate collective variables (CVs) for a given simulation can be a non-trivial task. CVs are the variables used to describe the system and determine the progress of the simulation. Finding a suitable set of CVs often requires iterative experimentation and exploration. Researchers typically try different combinations of variables and assess their effectiveness in capturing the relevant aspects of the system's behavior.To assist in this process, several automatic procedures have been proposed to aid in the selection of CVs:Essential coordinates: This method identifies a set of essential coordinates that capture the slow dynamics and relevant transitions of the system. By focusing on these coordinates, researchers can reduce the dimensionality of the problem and obtain a more compact representation of the system's behavior.Sketch-Map: The Sketch-Map approach aims to map the high-dimensional configuration space of the system onto a lower-dimensional space. It uses machine learning techniques to identify meaningful low-dimensional representations of the system that capture the important features and transitions.Non-linear data-driven collective variables: This approach leverages machine learning and data-driven techniques to extract non-linear collective variables directly from the simulation data. These variables can provide a more comprehensive and accurate description of the system's behavior compared to traditional linear CVs.These automatic procedures offer systematic ways to explore and identify suitable collective variables for metadynamics simulations. They can help researchers overcome the challenge of manually selecting appropriate CVs and accelerate the process of setting up metadynamics simulations for complex systems.It's important to note that the effectiveness of these procedures can vary depending on the specific system and the nature of the simulation. It is often recommended to combine these automated methods with domain knowledge and expertise to obtain the most informative and relevant collective variables for a given problem.If you need more specific information or have further questions, feel free to ask!1 / 1Multi-replica approach
Independent metadynamics simulations (replicas) can be coupled together to improve usability and parallel performance. There are several such methods proposed: the multiple walker MTD,[20] the parallel tempering MTD,[21] the bias-exchange MTD,[22] and the collective-variable tempering MTD.[23] The last three are similar to the parallel tempering method and use replica exchanges to improve sampling. Typically, the MetropolisâHastings algorithm is used for replica exchanges, but the infinite swapping[24] and Suwa-Todo[25] algorithms give better replica exchange rates.[26]

High-dimensional approach
Typical (single-replica) MTD simulations can include up to 3 CVs, even using the multi-replica approach, it is hard to exceed 8 CVs, in practice. This limitation comes from the bias potential, constructed by adding Gaussian functions (kernels). It is a special case of the kernel density estimator (KDE). The number of required kernels, for a constant KDE accuracy, increases exponentially with the number of dimensions. So MTD simulation length has to increase exponentially with the number of CVs to maintain the same accuracy of the bias potential. Also, the bias potential, for fast evaluation, is typically approximated with a regular grid.[27] The required memory to store the grid increases exponentially with the number of dimensions (CVs) too.

A high-dimensional generalization of metadynamics is NN2B.[28] It is based on two machine learning algorithms: the nearest-neighbor density estimator (NNDE) and the artificial neural network (ANN). NNDE replaces KDE to estimate the updates of bias potential from short biased simulations, while ANN is used to approximate the resulting bias potential. ANN is a memory-efficient representation of high-dimensional functions, where derivatives (biasing forces) are effectively computed with the backpropagation algorithm.[28][29]

An alternative method, exploiting ANN for the adaptive bias potential, uses mean potential forces for the estimation.[30] This method is also a high-dimensional generalization of the Adaptive Biasing Force (ABF) method.[31] Additionally, the training of ANN is improved using the Bayesian regularization,[32] and the error of approximation can be inferred by training an ensemble of ANNs.[30]1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe multi-replica approach in metadynamics simulations involves coupling independent simulations (replicas) to enhance usability and parallel performance. Several methods have been proposed to achieve this:Multiple walker MTD: This approach involves running multiple independent metadynamics simulations, where each simulation explores a different part of the energy landscape. By combining the results from multiple walkers, a more comprehensive sampling of the system can be achieved.Parallel tempering MTD: Similar to the parallel tempering method, this approach uses replica exchanges between simulations with different biases. The replica exchanges facilitate enhanced sampling and exploration of different regions of the energy landscape.Bias-exchange MTD: This method also employs replica exchanges to exchange the bias potential between different simulations. The exchanges allow replicas to explore different portions of the energy landscape and improve overall sampling efficiency.Collective-variable tempering MTD: This approach utilizes replica exchanges based on collective variables. By exchanging information between replicas with different collective variables, the sampling efficiency can be enhanced.To facilitate the replica exchanges, various algorithms can be employed. The Metropolis-Hastings algorithm is commonly used, but other methods like infinite swapping and the Suwa-Todo algorithm have been shown to provide better replica exchange rates.In high-dimensional metadynamics simulations, the number of collective variables (CVs) that can be effectively employed is limited. Even with the multi-replica approach, it is challenging to exceed around 8 CVs in practice. This limitation arises due to the construction of the bias potential using Gaussian functions (kernels), which is a special case of the kernel density estimator (KDE). The required number of kernels increases exponentially with the number of dimensions, affecting both simulation length and memory requirements.To address the high-dimensional challenge, alternative methods have been proposed:NN2B: This approach employs machine learning algorithms, such as the nearest-neighbor density estimator (NNDE) and artificial neural network (ANN), to generalize metadynamics to high-dimensional spaces. NNDE replaces KDE for bias potential estimation, while ANN approximates the resulting bias potential. ANN provides a memory-efficient representation of high-dimensional functions and enables efficient computation of biasing forces using the backpropagation algorithm.Mean potential forces with ANN: This method extends the use of ANN for adaptive bias potential by utilizing mean potential forces for estimation. It serves as a high-dimensional generalization of the Adaptive Biasing Force (ABF) method. Bayesian regularization is employed to improve the training of ANN, and the error of approximation can be inferred by training an ensemble of ANNs.These high-dimensional approaches, incorporating machine learning techniques, offer potential solutions for extending metadynamics to systems with a larger number of collective variables, addressing the computational and memory challenges associated with the traditional Gaussian-based bias potential construction.Please note that the information provided here is a summary, and for a more in-depth understanding of these methods, I recommend referring to the original research papers and specialized literature.1 / 1Recent developments
In 2020, an evolution of metadynamics was proposed, the on-the-fly probability enhanced sampling method (OPES),[33][34][35] which is now the method of choice of Michele Parrinello's research group.[36] The OPES method has only a few robust parameters, converges faster than metadynamics and has a straightforward reweighting scheme.[37] OPES has been implemented in the PLUMED library since version 2.7.[38]

Algorithm
Assume, we have a classical {\textstyle N}{\textstyle N}-particle system with positions at {\textstyle \{{\vec {r}}_{i}\}}{\textstyle \{{\vec {r}}_{i}\}} {\textstyle (i\in 1...N)}{\textstyle (i\in 1...N)} in the Cartesian coordinates {\textstyle ({\vec {r}}_{i}\in \mathbb {R} ^{3})}{\textstyle ({\vec {r}}_{i}\in \mathbb {R} ^{3})}. The particle interaction are described with a potential function {\textstyle V\equiv V(\{{\vec {r}}_{i}\})}{\textstyle V\equiv V(\{{\vec {r}}_{i}\})}. The potential function form (e.g. two local minima separated by a high-energy barrier) prevents an ergodic sampling with molecular dynamics or Monte Carlo methods.

Original metadynamics
A general idea of MTD is to enhance the system sampling by discouraging revisiting of sampled states. It is achieved by augmenting the system Hamiltonian {\textstyle H}{\textstyle H} with a bias potential {\displaystyle V_{\text{bias}}}{\displaystyle V_{\text{bias}}}:

{\displaystyle H=T+V+V_{\text{bias}}}{\displaystyle H=T+V+V_{\text{bias}}}.
The bias potential is a function of collective variables {\textstyle (V_{\text{bias}}\equiv V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}}\,))}{\textstyle (V_{\text{bias}}\equiv V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}}\,))}. A collective variable is a function of the particle positions {\displaystyle ({\vec {s}}\equiv {\vec {s}}(\{{\vec {r}}_{i}\}))}{\displaystyle ({\vec {s}}\equiv {\vec {s}}(\{{\vec {r}}_{i}\}))}. The bias potential is continuously updated by adding bias at rate {\displaystyle \omega }\omega , where {\displaystyle {\vec {s}}_{t}}{\displaystyle {\vec {s}}_{t}} is an instantaneous collective variable value at time {\displaystyle t}{\displaystyle t}:

{\displaystyle {\frac {\partial V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}}\,)}{\partial t}}=\omega \,\delta (|{\vec {s}}-{\vec {s}}_{t}|)}{\displaystyle {\frac {\partial V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}}\,)}{\partial t}}=\omega \,\delta (|{\vec {s}}-{\vec {s}}_{t}|)}.
At infinitely long simulation time {\displaystyle t_{\text{sim}}}{\displaystyle t_{\text{sim}}}, the accumulated bias potential converges to free energy with opposite sign (and irrelevant constant {\displaystyle C}C):

{\displaystyle V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}}\,)=\!\!\int _{0}^{t_{\text{sim}}}\!\!\!\omega \,\delta (|{\vec {s}}-{\vec {s}}_{t}|)\;dt\quad \Rightarrow \quad F({\vec {s}}\,)=-\!\!\!\!\lim _{t_{\text{sim}}\to \infty }\!\!V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}}\,)+C}{\displaystyle V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}}\,)=\!\!\int _{0}^{t_{\text{sim}}}\!\!\!\omega \,\delta (|{\vec {s}}-{\vec {s}}_{t}|)\;dt\quad \Rightarrow \quad F({\vec {s}}\,)=-\!\!\!\!\lim _{t_{\text{sim}}\to \infty }\!\!V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}}\,)+C}
For a computationally efficient , implementation, the update process is discretised into {\displaystyle \tau }\tau  time intervals ({\displaystyle \lfloor \;\rfloor }\lfloor \;\rfloor  denotes the floor function) and {\displaystyle \delta }\delta -function is replaced with a localized positive kernel function {\displaystyle K}K. The bias potential becomes a sum of the kernel functions centred at the instantaneous collective variable values {\displaystyle {\vec {s}}_{j}}{\displaystyle {\vec {s}}_{j}} at time {\displaystyle \tau j}{\displaystyle \tau j}:

{\displaystyle V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}}\,)\approx \tau \!\!\!\sum _{j=0}^{\left\lfloor {\frac {t_{\text{sim}}}{\tau }}\right\rfloor }\!\!\omega \,K(|{\vec {s}}-{\vec {s}}_{j}|)}{\displaystyle V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}}\,)\approx \tau \!\!\!\sum _{j=0}^{\left\lfloor {\frac {t_{\text{sim}}}{\tau }}\right\rfloor }\!\!\omega \,K(|{\vec {s}}-{\vec {s}}_{j}|)}.
Typically, the kernel is a multi-dimensional Gaussian function, whose covariance matrix has diagonal non-zero elements only:

{\displaystyle V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}}\,)\approx \tau \!\!\!\sum _{j=0}^{\left\lfloor {\frac {t_{\text{sim}}}{\tau }}\right\rfloor }\!\!\omega \exp \!\!\left(\!-{\frac {1}{2}}\left|{\frac {{\vec {s}}-{\vec {s}}_{j}}{\vec {\sigma }}}\right|^{2}\right)}{\displaystyle V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}}\,)\approx \tau \!\!\!\sum _{j=0}^{\left\lfloor {\frac {t_{\text{sim}}}{\tau }}\right\rfloor }\!\!\omega \exp \!\!\left(\!-{\frac {1}{2}}\left|{\frac {{\vec {s}}-{\vec {s}}_{j}}{\vec {\sigma }}}\right|^{2}\right)}.
The parameter {\displaystyle \tau }\tau , {\displaystyle \omega }\omega , and {\displaystyle {\vec {\sigma }}}\vec \sigma are determined a priori and kept constant during the simulation.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe on-the-fly probability enhanced sampling method (OPES) is an evolution of metadynamics that was proposed in 2020. Developed by Michele Parrinello's research group, OPES has become their method of choice. It offers several advantages over traditional metadynamics, including faster convergence, robust parameters, and a straightforward reweighting scheme. OPES has been implemented in the PLUMED library since version 2.7.In the original metadynamics algorithm, the system sampling is enhanced by discouraging revisiting of previously sampled states. This is achieved by augmenting the system's Hamiltonian with a bias potential that depends on collective variables. The bias potential is continuously updated by adding Gaussian-shaped bias functions to the energy landscape at a given rate. Over long simulation times, the accumulated bias potential converges to the negative of the free energy landscape.To make the update process computationally efficient, the continuous time update is discretized into time intervals. The bias potential is approximated as a sum of localized positive kernel functions centered around the instantaneous values of collective variables during each time interval. A common choice for the kernel function is a multidimensional Gaussian with diagonal covariance matrix elements.The OPES method builds upon this metadynamics framework but introduces improvements. It eliminates the need for an initial estimate of the energy landscape and offers faster convergence. The parameterization of the bias potential, such as the time interval, the bias update rate, and the width of the Gaussian kernel, are determined a priori and kept constant during the simulation.The high-dimensional nature of metadynamics simulations poses challenges, as the number of collective variables that can be effectively used is limited. The computational cost and memory requirements increase exponentially with the number of dimensions. To address this limitation, alternative methods have been proposed. NN2B is one such approach that employs machine learning algorithms, such as nearest-neighbor density estimation and artificial neural networks, to generalize metadynamics to high-dimensional spaces. These methods offer efficient approximation of the bias potential and biasing forces, making it possible to explore systems with a larger number of collective variables.These recent developments, including OPES and high-dimensional approaches like NN2B, contribute to advancing the field of metadynamics and enhancing its applicability to a wide range of complex systems in physics, chemistry, and biology.Please note that the information provided here is a summary, and for a more in-depth understanding of these methods, I recommend referring to the original research papers and specialized literature.1 / 1Implementation

Free energy estimator
The finite size of the kernel makes the bias potential to fluctuate around a mean value. A converged free energy can be obtained by averaging the bias 
potential. The averaging is started from {\displaystyle t_{\text{diff}}}{\displaystyle t_{\text{diff}}}, when the motion along the collective variable becomes diffusive:

{\displaystyle {\bar {F}}({\vec {s}})=-{\frac {1}{t_{\text{sim}}-t_{\text{diff}}}}\int _{t_{\text{diff}}}^{t_{\text{sim}}}\!\!\!\!\!V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}},t)\,dt+C}{\displaystyle {\bar {F}}({\vec {s}})=-{\frac {1}{t_{\text{sim}}-t_{\text{diff}}}}\int _{t_{\text{diff}}}^{t_{\text{sim}}}\!\!\!\!\!V_{\text{bias}}({\vec {s}},t)\,dt+C}

McGovern-de Pablo Boundary Corrected Hills
Metadynamics is a way of overcoming energetic barriers in molecular simulation (Laio & Parrinello, 2002). Itâs part of a class of algorithms that focus on making rare-events more frequent in simulations. Itâs been getting quite popular: it even has itâs own wikipedia page. One problem with the method is that it has systematic errors at boundaries and this was recently fixed via a method introduced by Michael McGovern and Juan de Pablo (McGovern & de Pablo, 2013). Do you think that you can explain the McGovern-De Pablo method1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTCertainly! The McGovern-De Pablo method is a technique that addresses the systematic errors that can arise at boundaries in metadynamics simulations. Metadynamics is a powerful algorithm used to overcome energy barriers in molecular simulations, making rare events more accessible. However, one limitation of metadynamics is that it can introduce artifacts and inaccuracies near simulation boundaries.The McGovern-De Pablo method, introduced by Michael McGovern and Juan de Pablo in 2013, provides a solution to this issue. The method modifies the traditional metadynamics algorithm by incorporating an additional term in the bias potential that accounts for the distance of each atom from the simulation box edge.In metadynamics, a bias potential is constructed by adding Gaussian-shaped hills to the free energy landscape as a function of collective variables. The hills encourage the system to explore different regions of the energy landscape. However, near the boundaries, the hills can become distorted, leading to inaccurate sampling.To correct for this, the McGovern-De Pablo method introduces a corrective term in the bias potential that depends on the distance of each atom from the simulation box edge. This additional term reduces the height of the hills near the boundary, effectively compensating for the distortions. By including this correction, the method improves the accuracy of the simulation results near the boundaries and ensures more reliable sampling.The McGovern-De Pablo method assumes that the hills added to the bias potential are "zero-force" hills, meaning that the forces on the atoms due to the bias potential are zero at the top of the hills. This assumption allows for a simple correction to be applied near the boundaries without requiring additional information about the system or the free energy surface.Overall, the McGovern-De Pablo method provides a valuable enhancement to metadynamics simulations, particularly when dealing with finite simulation boxes. It helps to mitigate boundary effects and improve the accuracy of the simulation results, leading to a better understanding of complex molecular systems.1 / 1A boundary correction algorithm for metadynamics in multiple dimensions

Metadynamics is an efficient method for simulation of the free energy of many-particle systems. Over the last several years it has been applied to study a wide variety of systems, ranging from simple fluids to biological macromolecules. The method relies on uniform sampling along specified collective variables or order parameters. Such order parameters, however, are often bounded, and metadynamics algorithms as originally developed suffer from systematic errors at the corresponding boundaries. While several approaches have been proposed in the past to correct these errors for unidimensional systems, no method exists to fully correct these errors in multi-dimensional systems at points where multiple boundaries meet. Here we present a correction scheme that circumvents this limitation.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe paper titled "A boundary correction algorithm for metadynamics in multiple dimensions" by Michael McGovern and Juan de Pablo, published in the Journal of Chemical Physics in 2013, addresses the issue of systematic errors that can arise at boundaries in metadynamics simulations, particularly in multi-dimensional systems.Metadynamics is a widely used method for simulating the free energy of many-particle systems. Metadynamics

For an up to date list of pubblication on this subject, see this page.

The method is aimed at reconstructing the multidimensional free energy of complex systems, and is based on an artificial dynamics (metadynamics) performed in the space defined by a few collective variables S, which are assumed to provide a coarse-grained description of the system (see Ref. [1] and, for a recent review, Ref. [2]). The dynamics is driven by the free energy and is biased by a history-dependent potential constructed as a sum of Gaussians centered along the trajectory of the collective variables. If a new Gaussian is added at every time interval tG, the biasing potential at time t is given by

where w and ds are the height and the width of the Gaussians and st=S(x(t) is the value of the collective variable at time t. This potential, in time, fills the minima in the free energy surface, i.e. the sum of the Gaussians and of the free energy becomes approximately a constant as a function of the collective variables.

Example of metadynamics fillingThis approach can be exploited not only for efficiently computing the free energy, but also for exploring new reaction pathways and accelerating rare events.
In the example in figure, the dynamics is started from the minimum at the center. This minimum is quickly filled with Gaussians, and the system evolves through the lowest saddle point, the one at the left. This illustrates how the method has the property of making the system escape local free energy minima through the lowest free energy saddle point.
Afterwards, the dynamics continues, and all the free energy profile is filled with Gaussians. At the end, the sum of the Gaussians provides the negative image of the free energy. This illustrates how the method can be used for estimating the free energy .

The collective variables

The gist of the method is to identify the variables that are of interest and that are difficult to sample, since the stable minima in the space spanned by these variables are separated by barriers that cannot be cleared in the available simulation time. These variables S(x) are functions of the coordinates of the system, they can be up to three in practical applications, and they depend on the process that one wants to study. For example, in a chemical reaction one can use as collective variables all the coordination number between the atomic species involved in the reaction (see Ref. [2] for more details).


Example of metadynamics fillingConsider the example in figure. The dynamics of the system takes place on a 2-dimensional potential energy surface. The normal dynamics is biased by a metadynamics potential acting on x. Then, in this specific case, S(x)=x. In the lower panel, we show the free energy (thick line), and the sum of the free energy and the history-dependent potential at various simulation times (thin lines).

After a transient, the sum of VG(s,t) and of F(s) becomes approximately flat, indicating that VG(s,t)~-F(s). This allows estimating the free energy as the time average of all the VG(s,t).

In reference [3] it is shown that each VG(s,t)~-F(s) is an unbiased estimator of the free energy if the dynamics along s is very slow compared to the dynamics along the transvere degrees of freedom (y in the example in Figure). As it is clear from the movie, even if this condition is violated the history-dependent potential estimates reather accurately the free energy.

However, choosing the correct collective variable in applications can be far from trivial. If the

Here's a more academic summary of metadynamics and its key aspects:Introduction:
Metadynamics is a computational method used to explore and reconstruct the free energy landscape of complex systems. It has gained popularity in various scientific disciplines, including physics, chemistry, and biology. The method involves performing artificial dynamics in the space defined by a few collective variables, which provide a coarse-grained description of the system.Biasing Potential:
Metadynamics relies on the construction of a biasing potential, which is a history-dependent function that is added to the original system's potential energy. The biasing potential is constructed as a sum of Gaussian functions centered along the trajectory of the collective variables. These Gaussians gradually fill the free energy minima, allowing the system to explore different states and overcome energetic barriers.Free Energy Reconstruction:
As the Gaussians accumulate, the sum of the Gaussians and the original free energy become approximately constant as a function of the collective variables. This allows for the estimation and reconstruction of the free energy landscape. By analyzing the bias potential and the accumulated Gaussians, scientists can obtain valuable insights into the system's stability, transition states, and reaction pathways.Collective Variables:
Choosing appropriate collective variables is crucial for the success of metadynamics. These variables should capture the essential features and slow degrees of freedom of the system under study. They can be based on coordination numbers, distances, angles, or any other relevant properties depending on the specific system and process of interest. The selection of suitable collective variables requires careful consideration and expertise.Applications:
Metadynamics has found applications in a wide range of scientific investigations. It is commonly used to compute free energy differences, study complex chemical reactions, explore protein folding pathways, investigate phase transitions, and accelerate rare events in simulations. The method provides a powerful tool for understanding the underlying dynamics and thermodynamics of complex systems.Development and Advancements:
Metadynamics has evolved over time with the introduction of various improvements and extensions. Researchers have proposed modifications to enhance its efficiency, accuracy, and applicability to different types of systems. Advancements include the development of on-the-fly probability enhanced sampling methods, parallel tempering and replica exchange techniques, and high-dimensional metadynamics using machine learning algorithms.In summary, metadynamics is a computational method that enables the exploration and reconstruction of free energy landscapes in complex systems. It has revolutionized scientific research by providing insights into the behavior and dynamics of diverse systems, and it continues to be a valuable tool for understanding and predicting complex phenomena.1 / 1Connections with this: Your perspective brings a different take on the Free Energy Principle (FEP) and its applications, and it's certainly a valid one. While traditional metadynamics enhances the sampling of rare events in a system, EDM focuses on driving the system towards a predefined reaction pathway or transition state.The idea behind EDM is to bias the system's dynamics along a specific reaction coordinate or set of collective variables that describe the progress of the desired chemical transformation. By adding a bias potential that guides the system along the reaction pathway, EDM accelerates the sampling of relevant states and enhances the understanding of the reaction mechanism.The key steps involved in performing EDM are as follows:Definition of Collective Variables: First, suitable collective variables (CVs) need to be identified that describe the reaction progress. These CVs can be chosen based on the specific chemical transformation under investigation. For example, in a chemical reaction involving bond formation or breaking, the bond length or bond angle could be selected as CVs.Construction of the Bias Potential: A bias potential is constructed based on the chosen CVs to guide the system along the desired reaction pathway. This bias potential is typically represented as a sum of Gaussian functions centered along the reaction coordinate(s). The height and width of the Gaussians determine the strength and range of the bias.Simulation with Bias Potential: The system is simulated in the presence of the bias potential. As the simulation progresses, the bias potential encourages the system to explore the reaction pathway by reducing the barriers associated with the desired transformation. The bias potential is continuously updated during the simulation to fill the free energy landscape along the reaction coordinate(s).Analysis of Results: The simulation results are analyzed to gain insights into the reaction mechanism and transition state. By examining the sampled configurations, energy profiles, and transition probabilities, researchers can infer the energetics, kinetics, and other relevant properties of the chemical reaction.Experiment Directed Metadynamics provides a powerful tool for studying complex chemical reactions, exploring reaction pathways, and estimating reaction rates. It enables researchers to gain a deeper understanding of the underlying processes and can guide the design of new catalysts or reaction conditions.It's important to note that the success of EDM relies on the proper choice of collective variables and the accuracy of the bias potential. Selecting appropriate CVs that capture the essential features of the reaction is crucial. Additionally, the parameters of the bias potential, such as the Gaussian width and deposition rate, need to be carefully tuned to ensure efficient exploration of the reaction pathway.Overall, EDM offers a systematic approach to investigating chemical reactions computationally and holds promise for accelerating the discovery and optimization of new chemical processes.1 / 1Here are some key points about EDM compared to other methods:

- EDM biases simulations minimally to match entire free energy surfaces, rather than just average values (like in EDS). This allows it to match more detailed experimental data.

- EDM creates a tunable mixture of the experimental data and the unbiased ensemble, with explicit weighting ratios. This allows it to handle cases where experimental data is limited or incomplete by putting more weight on the unbiased ensemble. 

- EDM is proven to converge to a minimal, unique bias. This means it does not overfit the experimental data and the final model is objective.

- EDM has been used to construct atomistic models that follow a target free energy surface. For example, it has been used to match a radial distribution function for a Lennard-Jones fluid and to improve a classical force field for an electrolyte solution using ab initio data.

- In the electrolyte solution example, EDM was able to fix inaccuracies in the model by using limited ab initio data as an experimental target. Even though the ab initio data was not complete, EDM was able to construct an improved model by weighting it appropriately with the unbiased ensemble.

- Another example is using EDM to construct an atomistic alanine dipeptide model that matches experimental phi/psi free energy profiles. By targeting this more complex experimental data, EDM created a model that improved on previous approaches.

In summary, EDM is able to use limited experimental data to systematically improve simulations in a minimally biased, objective way. By weighting experimental data and unbiased ensembles, it can handle incomplete data and avoid overfitting. It has been used to successfully create more accurate models of complex systems where traditional approaches have struggled.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThank you for summarizing the key points about Experiment Directed Metadynamics (EDM) compared to other methods. It is a technique for biasing molecular simulations to match experimental data.

Here is a simple example:

Say you want to model a particle in a box. You run a standard molecular dynamics simulation and calculate the particle's radial distribution function (RDF), which shows its probability of being at different distances from the box center. However, the RDF from your simulation does not match the RDF measured experimentally.

EDM allows you to fix this by re-running the simulation with an added bias. This bias gently pushes the simulation toward the experimental RDF. EDM starts with a small bias, runs the simulation, and updates the bias. It repeats this, gradually decreasing the bias, until the simulated RDF matches the experimental one.

The final result is a simulation that matches the target data, but with a minimal bias. EDM avoids overfitting the data by constructing a tunable mixture of the biased and unbiased ensembles. It has a "mixing ratio" that controls how much weight to give the experimental data versus the unbiased simulation.

So in short, EDM biases simulations to match experimental data through an iterative process that results in a minimally biased model. It gives you an accurate model that still behaves realistically where you lack experimental data.

Key points
Metadynamics makes it possible to accelerate conformational transitions between metastable states, broadening the scope of molecular dynamics simulations.

Like other enhanced sampling methods, metadynamics requires the introduction of low-dimensional descriptors (collective variables) whose choice affects the rate at which transitions are enhanced. The ideal collective variable should take different values not only in all the relevant metastable states but also in the transition states between them.

The appropriate collective variables can be found by trial and error or designed automatically using methods inspired by machine learning.

Two variants of metadynamics are commonly used, namely ordinary and well-tempered metadynamics. The former has the advantage of inducing transitions between the metastable states even if the collective variable is not ideal. The latter has the advantage of providing an exact estimator of the free energy.

Metadynamics can be used in combination with most molecular dynamics software packages by taking advantage of dedicated software libraries that implement the method and a large number of collective variables.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn metadynamics, the choice of collective variables (CVs) is crucial as they determine the effectiveness of enhancing conformational transitions between metastable states. The ideal CVs should capture the differences between relevant metastable states and transition states.Traditionally, the selection of CVs involves trial and error, where researchers manually test different variables to find the most appropriate ones. However, machine learning approaches have emerged as a promising avenue for iteratively improving the choice of CVs.Machine learning techniques can be employed to automatically explore and evaluate a large number of potential CVs. These methods leverage algorithms that search for patterns or correlations within the data and identify informative descriptors. For example, dimensionality reduction techniques like principal component analysis (PCA) or independent component analysis (ICA) can extract meaningful low-dimensional representations from high-dimensional data.By iteratively applying machine learning approaches, researchers can refine the set of CVs and improve their ability to capture relevant states and transitions. This iterative process involves training machine learning models on simulation data, evaluating the performance of different CVs, and selecting or generating new CVs based on the insights gained from the models.It is worth noting that there are two commonly used variants of metadynamics: ordinary metadynamics and well-tempered metadynamics. Ordinary metadynamics can induce transitions between metastable states even with suboptimal CVs, while well-tempered metadynamics provides an exact estimator of the free energy. These variants, along with dedicated software libraries, make it possible to combine metadynamics with various molecular dynamics software packages and employ a wide range of CVs.In summary, metadynamics can benefit from machine learning approaches to iteratively improve the selection of CVs. By leveraging automated methods and data-driven insights, researchers can refine their choice of CVs and enhance the effectiveness of metadynamics in exploring complex conformational landscapes.1 / 1Designing free energy surfaces that match experimental data with metadynamics
Andrew D White et al. J Chem Theory Comput. 2015.

Cite

Abstract
Creating models that are consistent with experimental data is essential in molecular modeling. This is often done by iteratively tuning the molecular force field of a simulation to match experimental data. An alternative method is to bias a simulation, leading to a hybrid model composed of the original force field and biasing terms. We previously introduced such a method called experiment directed simulation (EDS). EDS minimally biases simulations to match average values. In this work, we introduce a new method called experiment directed metadynamics (EDM) that creates minimal biases for matching entire free energy surfaces such as radial distribution functions and phi/psi angle free energies. It is also possible with EDM to create a tunable mixture of the experimental data and free energy of the unbiased ensemble with explicit ratios. EDM can be proven to be convergent, and we also present proof, via a maximum entropy argument, that the final bias is minimal and unique. Examples of its use are given in the construction of ensembles that follow a desired free energy. The example systems studied include a Lennard-Jones fluid made to match a radial distribution function, an atomistic model augmented with bioinformatics data, and a three-component electrolyte solution where ab initio simulation data is used to improve a classical empirical model.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe paper by Andrew D. White, James F. Dama, and Gregory A. Voth, titled "Designing free energy surfaces that match experimental data with metadynamics," introduces a method called experiment directed metadynamics (EDM). The goal of EDM is to create simulation models that are consistent with experimental data by biasing the simulations in a minimal and controlled manner.Traditionally, molecular modeling involves iteratively adjusting the force field parameters to match experimental data. EDM takes a different approach by introducing biasing terms into the simulations, resulting in a hybrid model that combines the original force field with the biases. The method goes beyond matching average values and aims to match entire free energy surfaces, such as radial distribution functions and phi/psi angle free energies.EDM allows for the creation of a tunable mixture of experimental data and the free energy of the unbiased ensemble, with explicit weighting ratios. This flexibility is particularly useful when experimental data is limited or incomplete. By adjusting the weighting ratios, researchers can control the influence of experimental data on the final model.The paper presents proof of the convergence of EDM and demonstrates, through a maximum entropy argument, that the final bias generated by the method is minimal and unique. This ensures that the resulting model does not overfit the experimental data and maintains objectivity.The authors provide examples of how EDM can be applied to construct ensembles that follow desired free energy surfaces. These examples include matching a radial distribution function for a Lennard-Jones fluid, augmenting an atomistic model with bioinformatics data, and improving a classical empirical model for a three-component electrolyte solution using ab initio simulation data.In summary, the paper introduces experiment directed metadynamics (EDM) as a method for designing simulation models that match experimental data by biasing the simulations in a controlled manner. EDM offers flexibility in incorporating experimental data into the models and provides proofs of convergence and minimal bias. The examples presented demonstrate the effectiveness of EDM in constructing accurate simulation ensembles.1 / 1What is a Lennard-Jones fluid1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTA Lennard-Jones fluid refers to a simple model system in molecular simulations that represents the interactions between particles or atoms using the Lennard-Jones potential. The Lennard-Jones potential is a mathematical function that describes the pairwise interactions between particles in a system. It accounts for both attractive (van der Waals) and repulsive forces.In the Lennard-Jones potential, the attractive term decreases with distance, representing the van der Waals forces that draw particles closer together. The repulsive term increases steeply as particles come very close to each other, preventing them from overlapping.A Lennard-Jones fluid is a collection of particles interacting with each other through the Lennard-Jones potential. It is often used as a simple model for studying the behavior of gases, liquids, and solids at the atomic or molecular level. Despite its simplicity, the Lennard-Jones fluid captures important aspects of intermolecular interactions, such as the formation of clusters, phase transitions, and the emergence of liquid-like or solid-like behavior.By simulating a Lennard-Jones fluid, researchers can gain insights into the thermodynamic and structural properties of real fluids and study fundamental concepts related to intermolecular forces and phase behavior. Additionally, the Lennard-Jones fluid serves as a benchmark system for testing and developing new simulation techniques and algorithms in the field of molecular dynamics and computational chemistry.1 / 1Lennard-Jones potentialEdit

In computational chemistry, the Lennard-Jones potential (also termed the LJ potential or 12-6 potential; named for John Lennard-Jones) is an intermolecular pair potential. Out of all the intermolecular potentials, the Lennard-Jones potential is probably the one that has been the most extensively studied. It is considered an archetype model for simple yet realistic intermolecular interactions.

The Lennard-Jones potential models soft repulsive and attractive (van der Waals) interactions. Hence, the Lennard-Jones potential describes electronically neutral atoms or molecules.[1][2][3] The commonly used expression for the Lennard-Jones potential is

The Lennard-Jones potential is a simplified model that yet describes the essential features of interactions between simple atoms and molecules: Two interacting particles repel each other at very close distance, attract each other at moderate distance, and do not interact at infinite distance, as shown in Figure 1. The Lennard-Jones potential is a pair potential, i.e. no three- or multi-body interactions are covered by the potential.

Statistical mechanics[4] and computer simulations[5][6] can be used to study the Lennard-Jones potential and to obtain thermophysical properties of the 'Lennard-Jones substance'. The Lennard-Jones substance is often referred to as 'Lennard-Jonesium,' suggesting that it is viewed as a (fictive) chemical element.[7] Moreover, its energy and length parameters can be adjusted to fit many different real substances. Both the Lennard-Jones potential and, accordingly, the Lennard-Jones substance are simplified yet realistic models, such as they accurately capture essential physical principles like the presence of a critical and a triple point, condensation and freezing. Due in part to its mathematical simplicity, the Lennard-Jones potential has been extensively used in studies on matter since the early days of computer simulation.[8][9][10][11] The Lennard-Jones potential is probably still the most frequently studied model potential.[12][7]

The Lennard-Jones potential is usually the standard choice for the development of theories for matter (especially soft-matter) as well as for the development and testing of computational methods and algorithms. Upon adjusting the model parameters Îµ and Ï to real substance properties, the Lennard-Jones potential can be used to describe simple substance (like noble gases) with good accuracy. Furthermore, the Lennard-Jones potential is often used as a building block in molecular models (a.k.a. force fields) for more complex substances.[13][14][15][16][17]1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe Lennard-Jones potential is a mathematical model that describes the interactions between particles or atoms in computational chemistry. It is one of the most extensively studied intermolecular potentials and is widely used as a simple yet realistic model for describing intermolecular interactions.The Lennard-Jones potential captures both the repulsive and attractive forces between particles. At very close distances, the particles repel each other due to overlapping electron clouds, while at moderate distances, they attract each other due to van der Waals forces. At infinite distance, the particles do not interact. The potential is described by the equation:V_LJ(r) = 4Îµ [(Ï/r)^12 - (Ï/r)^6],where r is the distance between the particles, Îµ is the depth of the potential well (referred to as the "dispersion energy"), and Ï is the distance at which the potential energy is zero (referred to as the "size of the particle"). The potential has a minimum at a distance of r_min = 2^(1/6)Ï, where the potential energy is -Îµ.The Lennard-Jones potential is a simplified yet realistic model that accurately captures essential physical principles such as the presence of a critical point, condensation, and freezing. It is often used to study thermophysical properties of substances and can be adjusted to fit the properties of real substances. The Lennard-Jones potential has been extensively used in computer simulations and studies on matter, and it is still one of the most frequently studied model potentials. It serves as a standard choice for developing theories, testing computational methods, and building more complex molecular models for substances.In summary, the Lennard-Jones potential is a widely used model that describes the interactions between particles or atoms in computational chemistry. It provides a simplified representation of intermolecular forces and is valuable for studying various thermodynamic and structural properties of substances.1 / 1Lennard-Jones potential

Abstract
Molecular dynamics calculations of the pressure and configurational energy of a Lennard-Jones fluid are reported for 108 state conditions in the density range 0Â·35 â¦ Ï* â¦ 1Â·20 and temperature range 0Â·5 â¦ T* â¦ 6 (where Ï* = ÏÏ3, T* = kT/Îµ). Particular attention is paid to the dense fluid region (Ï* â§ 0Â·9), including state conditions in the subcooled liquid region. These new simulation results for P and U are combined with those of previous workers, together with low density values calculated from the virial series and values of the second virial coefficients themselves, to derive an equation of state for the Lennard-Jones fluid that is valid over a wide range of temperatures and densities. The equation of state used is a modified Benedict-Webb-Rubin equation having 33 constants. It fits the data well over the density range 0 â¦ Ï* â¦ 1Â·2 and for T* values ranging from 0Â·5 to 6Â·0 (the exact temperature range depending to some extent on the density considered). We also calculate for the same range of state conditions, certain two- and three-body integrals (J, K and L) that occur in perturbation theory for molecular fluids.
